SIBs: (a,c,d) EIS plots and (b,e) resistances of fresh and cycled cells.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/asset/images/large/am4c09246_0015.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09246&amp;id=fig15"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><figure id="fig16" data-index="16" class="article__inlineFigure"><h2 class="fig-label">Figure 16</h2><button class="figure-viewer__trigger" aria-label="open figure viewer"><img src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/asset/images/medium/am4c09246_0016.gif" alt="" data-lg-src="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/asset/images/large/am4c09246_0016.jpeg" id="gr18" class="inline-fig internalNav"></button><figcaption><div class="hlFld-FigureCaption caption"><p class="first last">Figure 16. SIBs: (a) CV, (b) log(<i>i</i>) vs log(<i>v</i>) plots; pseudocapacitive values at (c) 0.4 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and (d) different scan rates.</p></div></figcaption><div class="figure-bottom-links"><div class="download-hi-res-img"><a title="High Resolution Image" href="/cms/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/asset/images/large/am4c09246_0016.jpeg"><span>High Resolution Image</span></a></div><div class="download-ppt"><a title="Download MS PowerPoint Slide" href="/action/downloadFigures?doi=10.1021/acsami.4c09246&amp;id=fig16"><span>Download MS PowerPoint Slide</span></a></div></div></figure><div class="NLM_p">MOFs are formed by organic ligands and metal ions or with abundant voids and cavities, which can provide more storage sites for Li<sup>+</sup>, and also alleviate the volume change during charge and discharge. In addition, MOFs are rich in metal ions or clusters, so that MOF substitutes can obtain metal/metal oxide materials with large specific surface areas and porous structures under suitable calcination conditions. These MOF-derived materials can inherit the advantages of MOFs, including inherent morphology, high surface area, and adjustable porosity. Recently, MOFs have attracted much attention for applications in energy storage and have proven to be promising precursors for constructing various nanostructured electrodes. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref16 ref17 ref18 ref19" aria-label="reference 16 to 19">(16−19)</a> Zhang et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref20'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref20" aria-label="reference 20">(20)</a> have prepared nitrogen-doped graphene particles by pyrolyzing ZIF-8 polyhedrons. As a lithium battery anode material, the PNCs@Gr electrode maintained a high capacity of 2132 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 50 cycles at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> and a capacity of 785 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after 1000 cycles at 5 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Gao et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref21'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref21" aria-label="reference 21">(21)</a> have wrapped SnO<sub>2</sub>@MOF composite material in graphene as a negative electrode material for lithium-ion batteries. Due to abundant electroactive sites, short ion diffusion pathways, and superior electronic conductivity, the specific capacity of 450 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is still maintained after 1000 cycles at 1A g<sup>–1</sup>. Dai et al. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref22'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref22" aria-label="reference 22">(22)</a> have prepared MOF-derived materials (Nd-cMOF/ZIF-CFs) with novel structures by electrospinning. ZIF-CFs provided electrical conductivity and porous structure, while Nd-cMOF provided interfacial dynamic activity, electrical conductivity, sufficient space, and volume buffer. Therefore, as a sodium-ion battery anode, it shows excellent electrochemical stability, with a specific capacity of 480.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.05 A g<sup>–1</sup> and a capacity retention rate of 84% after 500 cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p">A large number of studies indicate that thermal runaway (TR) is the essential issue of batteries. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref23 ref24 ref25 ref26 ref27 ref28 ref29" aria-label="reference 23 to 29">(23−29)</a> Typically, the solvents with low flash points used in electrolytes are composed of highly combustible organic ester and ether compounds. Under abusive conditions, chemical exothermic reactions can easily occur between the active electrode material and the electrolyte. When the heat production rate of the battery is greater than the rate of heat dissipation to the environment, the heat inside the battery continues to accumulate, resulting in a continuous increase in the system temperature. When the chemical reaction rate and battery temperature reach the threshold, the TR of the battery occurs, releasing a large amount of heat and toxic combustible gases in a very short time and further evolving into combustion or explosion accidents. With the gradually increased energy density, safety has become at the forefront of current energy storage research. The design and use of flame-retardant electrolyte additives <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref30 ref31 ref32'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref30 ref31 ref32" aria-label="reference 30 to 32">(30−32)</a> (phosphates and fluorinated compounds), noncombustible electrolytes, <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref33 ref34'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref33 ref34" aria-label="reference 33 and 34">(33,34)</a> and modified separators <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref35 ref36 ref37 ref38" aria-label="reference 35 to 38">(35−38)</a> have been extensively studied. However, these improvements often come with electrochemical performance trade-offs. Therefore, achieving simultaneous promotions in electrochemical and safety performances has become an urgent issue. In this context, developing a safe anode with high energy density has been a great choice.</div><div class="NLM_p last">In this contribution, a heterogeneous engineering and carbon confinement strategy is employed to transform MOFs into metal selenides encased in a carbon substrate. The acquired product Ni–Se@G@C is further utilized as anodes. When used in LIBs, the cell delivers an initial discharge capacity of 936.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Even when running at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for 1000 cycles, a high capacity of 626.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is discharged. When used in SIBs, the cell shows an initial discharge capacity of 624.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even after 200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the cell still delivers a high capacity of 269.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Compared with cells with graphite anodes, cells with Ni–Se@G@C anodes show an obviously delayed TR process and promoted TR <i>E</i><sub>a</sub>. These results strongly indicate the efficacy of the Ni–Se@G@C anode in promoting the electrochemical properties and safety of cells.</div></div><div id="sec2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i5">2.  Experimental Section</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">The detailed information about reagents, synthesis, and characterizations is given in the <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Supporting Information</a>.</div></div><div id="sec3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i6">3.  Results and Discussion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div id="sec3_1" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i7" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i7">3.1.  Preparation and Structural Analysis</h3><div class="NLM_p">The preparation scheme of Ni–Se@G@C is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig1" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 1</a>. First, Ni-MOF nanospheres are synthesized by the solvothermal reaction. The corresponding scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>a,b. Of note, these nanospheres tend to accumulate to form microspheres. Subsequently, graphene oxide with a high aspect ratio and specific surface area is utilized to encapsulate Ni-MOF microspheres, through a hydrothermal reaction, to obtain the hybrid of rGO-Ni-MOF. Finally, the mixture is calcined in an argon environment to complete the selenization and carbonization process. Then, the target product of Ni–Se@G@C with a porous structure is acquired, which ensures full contact between the electrolyte and active substance. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 2</a>e–g illustrates that NiSe<sub>2</sub> nanosheets wrapped by a porous carbon skeleton are evenly distributed on the graphene surface. The porous carbon structure increases the conductive path, shortens the diffusion and transport paths of ions, and significantly improves the electrochemical performance. Moreover, the porous carbon structure provides a larger specific surface area, improving the electrode’s ability to store active substances. SEM and transmission electron microscopy (TEM) elemental mapping spectra of Ni–Se@G@C are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig2" id="" class=" internalNav">Figures 2</a>h and <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">S1</a>, which provide the uniform distributions of C, N, O, Ni, and Se elements. The existence of spherical metal selenides is clearly demonstrated in the TEM elemental mapping spectrum.</div><div class="NLM_p last">The X-ray diffraction (XRD) pattern of Ni–Se@G@C is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S2</a>. The peaks at 29.9°, 33.6°, 36.9°, 42.8°, 50.7°, 55.6°, and 57.8° belong to the (200), (210), (211), (220), (311), (023), and (321) planes of NiSe<sub>2</sub> (JCPDF No. 65-1843), respectively. In addition, the peaks at 16.6°, 33.1°, 44.9°, 50.1°, and 50.5° are attributed to the (101), (112), (301), (020), and (310) planes of Ni<sub>3</sub>Se<sub>4</sub> (JCPDF No. 89-7162), respectively. To further investigate the chemical composition and valence state of Ni–Se@G@C, the XPS technique is utilized. The full X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>a) confirms the presence of C, N, O, Ni, and Se elements. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>d shows the Ni 2p spectrum. The peaks at 870.5 and 853.3 eV correspond to Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Ni<sup>2+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>, while the peaks at 873.6 and 855.3 eV pertain to Ni<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>1/2</sub> and Ni<sup>3+</sup> 2p<sub>3/2</sub>. Also, the peaks at 878.5 and 859.9 eV belong to satellite peaks. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref39 ref40 ref41'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref39 ref40 ref41" aria-label="reference 39 to 41">(39−41)</a> The peaks at 59.1, 56.3, and 55.5 eV in the Se 2p spectrum confirm the presence of SeO<sub><i>x</i></sub>, Se 3d<sub>2/3</sub>, and Se 3d<sub>5/2</sub>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref42'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref42" aria-label="reference 42">(42)</a> The C 1s spectrum is given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>b, and the peaks at 288.3, 285.8, and 284.8 eV correspond to sp<sup>2</sup> bonded carbon, sp<sup>3</sup> bonded carbon, and sp<sup>2</sup> carbon satellite peak. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref43 ref44'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref43 ref44" aria-label="reference 43 and 44">(43,44)</a> In the N 1s spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>c), the presence of graphitic N, pyrrolic N, and pyridinic N can be observed at 400.1, 399.4, and 398.4 eV, which also proves the successful doping of the N atom. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref45'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref45" aria-label="reference 45">(45)</a> The proportion of N-Q is significantly higher than that of <i>N</i>-5 and <i>N</i>-6, indicating that the doped N atom mainly exists in the graphitized carbon layer rather than the outer layer. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref46'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref46" aria-label="reference 46">(46)</a> The presence of N-Q can enhance the conductivity of the material, thereby improving the electrochemical performance of the battery. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref47'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref47" aria-label="reference 47">(47)</a> The peaks at 532.3 and 398.4 eV in the O 1s (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>f) spectrum correspond to C–OH/M–OH and M-O, respectively. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref48'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref48" aria-label="reference 48">(48)</a> Compared with C atoms, N and Se atoms have different sizes and electronegativity. Doping of these heteroatoms will cause topological defects inside the material, thereby improving its electrochemical activity. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref49'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref49" aria-label="reference 49">(49)</a> The XPS technique is further utilized to investigate the chemical composition and valence state of NiSe<sub>2</sub>. The full X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) spectrum (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>g) confirms the presence of C, O, Ni, and Se elements. The C 1s spectrum of NiSe<sub>2</sub> is given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>h, and the peaks at 288.7, 286.2, and 284.6 eV correspond to sp<sup>2</sup> bonded carbon, sp<sup>3</sup> bonded carbon, and sp<sup>2</sup> carbon satellite peak. Raman spectroscopy was conducted to explore the composition of the carbon support in the hybrid (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig3" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 3</a>i), and the Raman spectra of Ni–Se@G@C were tested. The typical peak of the D band (1581.8 cm<sup>–1</sup>) is attributed to the existence of amorphous carbon sp<sup>3</sup>-like, while another peak, the G band (1349.4 cm<sup>–1</sup>), indicates the sp<sup>2</sup>-hybridized graphitic structure. The peak at 2704.9 cm<sup>–1</sup> corresponds to the 2D peak.</div></div><div id="sec3_2" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i8" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i8">3.2.  Thermal Hazard of LIBs</h3><div class="NLM_p">The TR process of LIBs is the result of mutual reaction coupling between the internal materials. In a high-temperature environment, these chemical reactions will be triggered and release a certain amount of heat to further accelerate the reaction rate. This process is usually referred to as a “self-accelerating” process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref50'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref50" aria-label="reference 50">(50)</a> The SEI decomposition, reaction between lithium embedded in the anode and electrolyte, and other chemical reactions generate less heat. However, it is difficult to accurately measure the heat in an open environment. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref51'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref51" aria-label="reference 51">(51)</a> Adiabatic acceleration calorimeter (ARC) is a thermal analysis instrument based on the principle of adiabatic design, which can control the temperature of the battery and its wall without destroying the battery structure, blocking the heat exchange between the battery and the environment, so as to ensure that the heat required by the battery from the “self-accelerating reaction” to the final comes from the battery itself. The TR evolution mechanism of LIBs can be accurately analyzed. The TR behaviors of half and full batteries with Ni–Se@G@C and graphite anodes are further conducted by EV-ARC (THT Inc.). Before the experiment, the battery of different samples is charged to the set state of charge after three precycles to ensure the integrity and capacity consistency of the battery. Then, the battery is suspended in the calorimetric chamber. A thermocouple is introduced into the tank through a port to measure the surface center temperature of the cells. An N-type thermocouple with a sensitivity of 0.01 °C is attached to the positive side of the battery with Teflon tape. Throughout the test, ARC heated the battery in a “heat-wait-search” mode. When the battery is self-exothermic and the heating rate exceeds 0.02 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, the heating process stops. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>a,b shows the temperature response and heating rate curves of the Li//Ni–Se@G@C half cell. The key TR parameters are given in <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl1" href="#tbl1">Table 1</a>. T<sub>0</sub>, <i>T</i><sub>1</sub>, and <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> are defined as the self-heating temperature, TR triggering temperature, and maximum TR temperature, respectively. <i>t</i><sub>0</sub>, <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>, and <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> are the corresponding times to <i>T</i><sub>0</sub>, <i>T</i><sub>1</sub>, and <i>T</i><sub>2</sub>. The results show that <i>T</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> of the Li//Ni–Se@G@C cell at 283.9 and 531.3 min are 200.8 and 387.3 °C, respectively. The <i>T</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> of the Li//graphite cell at 344.5 and 468.7 min are 222.7 and 300.3 °C, respectively. Clearly, <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> is increased by 87.0 °C. In addition, <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> is delayed from 506.9 to 546.5 min. These results confirm the improved thermal safety of the cell. Compared with graphite, Ni–Se@G@C shows a porous structure, which guarantees the more uniform deposition of Li in the electrode. In turn, the in situ formed carbon with a graphene layer can act as a protective shield to hinder the side reaction between the electrolyte and Li, reducing the intensity of the reaction. Moreover, the catalytic activity of metal selenide may disturb the TR reaction, resulting in the delay of TR. Notably, the use of the Ni–Se@G@C electrode results in a reduction in the maximum heating release rate (HRR) from 127.9 (Li//graphite cell) to 10.31 °C min<sup>–1</sup>, demonstrating the impaired severity of TR consequences.</div><div class="NLM_p">In the self-heating phase, all of the energy is generated by the exothermic reaction inside the battery. Assuming that the temperature rise follows the exponential form, the reaction kinetic parameters of the battery can be roughly calculated according to the Arrhenius law. The energy conservation equation can be expressed as <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref52 ref53 ref54 ref55" aria-label="reference 52 to 55">(52−55)</a><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq1"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-1"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-2"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-3"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-4"><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-5" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-6"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-7">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-8">T</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-9"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-10">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-11">t</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-12"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-13" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-14"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-15">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-16"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-17" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-18">T</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-19" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-20"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-21">ad</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-22">A</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-23" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-24">exp</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-25"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-26" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-27" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-28"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-29" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.111em;">−</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-30"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-31" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-32">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-33" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-34">a</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-35"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-36">R</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-37">T</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-38" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em; vertical-align: -0.689em;"><span class="MJXp-right MJXp-scale4" style="font-size: 3.756em; margin-left: -0.17em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-39" style="margin-left: 0.03em; margin-right: 0.03em;">·</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-40"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-41" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-42"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-43" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-44"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-45"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-46">1</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-47"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-48" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">−</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-49"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-50">a</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-51"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-52" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-53" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-54">n</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-1"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi>t</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mrow><mi>ad</mi></mrow></mrow></msub><mi>A</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.25em">⁡</mo><mi>exp</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="true">(</mo><mfrac><mrow><mo>−</mo><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi>R</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow></mfrac><mo stretchy="true">)</mo></mrow><mo lspace="0.03em" rspace="0.03em">·</mo><msup><mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo></mrow><mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mo>−</mo></mrow><mrow><mi>a</mi></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mrow><mrow><mi>n</mi></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(1)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">where <i>A</i> is the frequency factor, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is the activation energy, <i>a</i> is the degree of reaction, <i>n</i> is the reaction order, <i>R</i> is the gas constant, <i>T</i> is the temperature, and Δ<i>T</i><sub>ad</sub> is the adiabatic temperature rise (Δ<i>T</i><sub>ad</sub> = <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> – <i>T</i><sub>0</sub>). <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref55'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref55" aria-label="reference 55">(55)</a> At the beginning of the reaction, the reaction consumption is negligible, and after that, <i>a</i> is approximately 0. Then, (1 – <i>a</i>)<i><sup>n</sup></i> is roughly equal to 1. By taking the natural logarithm of both sides of <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq2" aria-label="eq 2">eq 2</a>, it is simplified to<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq2"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-55"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-56"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-57"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-58"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-59">ln</span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-60" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-61"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-62">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-63">T</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-64"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-65">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-66">t</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-67"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-68" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">≈</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-69"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-70">ln</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-71"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-72" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-73">Δ</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-74"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-75" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-76">T</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-77" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-78"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-79">ad</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-80" style="margin-left: 0.03em; margin-right: 0.03em;">·</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-81">A</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-82" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-83" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">−</span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-84" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-85"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-86"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-87" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-88">E</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-89" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-90">a</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-91"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-92">R</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-93">T</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-2"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>ln</mi><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi>t</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">≈</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>ln</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi mathvariant="normal">Δ</mi><msub><mrow><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mrow><mi>ad</mi></mrow></mrow></msub><mo lspace="0.03em" rspace="0.03em">·</mo><mi>A</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>−</mo><mfrac><mrow><msub><mrow><mi>E</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">a</mi></mrow></msub></mrow><mrow><mi>R</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow></mfrac></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(2)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">The fitted <i class="MathJaxEquation"></i><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math" id="MJXp-Span-94"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-95">ln</span><span class="MJXp-mfrac" id="MJXp-Span-96" style="vertical-align: 0.25em;"><span class="MJXp-box MJXp-script"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-97"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-98">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-99">T</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-box" style="margin-top: -0.9em;"><span class="MJXp-denom"><span><span class="MJXp-rule" style="height: 1em; border-top: none; border-bottom: 1px solid; margin: 0.1em 0px;"></span></span><span><span class="MJXp-box MJXp-script"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-100"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-101">d</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-102">t</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-3"><math display="inline"><mi>ln</mi><mfrac><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi>T</mi></mrow><mrow><mi mathvariant="normal">d</mi><mi>t</mi></mrow></mfrac></math></script> vs 1000/<i>T</i> curves of Li//graphite and Li//Ni–Se@G@C cells are given in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c. The calculated <i>E</i><sub><i>a</i></sub> values are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>d. Clearly, Li//graphite shows the low <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> value of 0.67 eV, which elevates to 1.51 eV after replacing it with the Ni–Se@G@C anode. Such a result indicates the improved intrinsic thermal safety of the battery.</div><div class="NLM_p last">TR behavior of lithium nickel cobalt manganate (NCM)//graphite and NCM//Ni–Se@G@C full cells is further evaluated, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig5" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 5</a>. Concretely, <i>T</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> of the NCM//graphite cell are 198.1 and 321.2 °C, respectively. The NCM//Ni–Se@G@C cell shows <i>T</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> values of 200.0 and 318.3 °C, respectively. In sharp contrast, <i>t</i><sub>0</sub>, <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>, and <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> are delayed by 11.5, 28.1, and 28.1 min, respectively. It is worth noting that the <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> of the NCM//Ni–Se@G@C battery is significantly reduced from 459.4 to 455.5 °C, indicating that the cell is less dangerous. In addition, the HRR<sub>max</sub> value of the NCM//Ni–Se@G@C cell is relatively gentle, giving a value of 6.6 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. In comparison, the NCM//graphite cell shows an HRR<sub>max</sub> of 11.7 °C min<sup>–1</sup>. These results jointly confirm that NCM//Ni–Se@G@C has higher thermal safety than the NCM//graphite cell. Similarly, with the replacement of the Ni–Se@G@C anode, <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is boosted from 0.68 to 0.94 eV, indicating that more activation energy is required to trigger TR of the Ni–Se@G@C cell. We have plotted the radar map of TR parameters of half and full cells in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig6" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 6</a>. It is intuitive that both the half and full cells with Ni–Se@G@C anodes show higher safety than those with graphite anodes.</div></div><div id="sec3_3" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i12" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i12">3.3.  Electrochemical Evaluation of LIBs</h3><div class="NLM_p">Lithium storage properties of Ni–Se@G@C are first investigated. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>a,b, at the current density of 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the initial discharge and charge-specific capacities of the Ni–Se@G@C battery are 921.5 and 652.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Meanwhile, a CE of 70.82% is observed. Such capacity loss derives from SEI film formation and other side reactions. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref56'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref56" aria-label="reference 56">(56)</a> With the deepening of charge and discharge behavior, the formation of good and stable SEI promoted the improvement of CE. Therefore, CE is &gt;94.0% after two cycles. In addition, the galvanostatic charge–discharge curve (GCD) shows good overlap, which further indicates the stability of the electrochemical performance. The discharge capacities at the 30th, 60th, 90th, 150th, and 200th cycles are 666.3, 764.6, 704.4, 793.9, and 998.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig4" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 4</a>c,d, the Ni–Se@G@C battery has initial discharge and charge capacities of 993.2 and 682.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively, showing a CE of 68.7%. The discharge capacities at the 100th, 200th, 300th, 400th, 500th, 600th, 700th, and 800th cycles are 333.7, 472.9, 500.7, 763.7, 981.1, 1176.3, 1337.3, and 1478.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Obviously, the discharge capacity gradually increases as the cycle progresses. Such a phenomenon can be associated with the gradual infiltration of electrodes during the long-term charging activation process. A stable dynamically activated polymer gel film is formed. At the same time, some Li<sup>+</sup> limited by the SEI film can dissociate back into the electrolyte and participate in the redox reaction of the electrode. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref57'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref57" aria-label="reference 57">(57)</a> It is worth noting that the Ni–Se@G@C electrode shows a good discharge platform after the first cycle, confirming its good electrochemical reversibility.</div><div class="NLM_p">Long-term cycling performance results of Ni–Se@G@C and NiSe<sub>2</sub> electrodes at 0.1–1 A g<sup>–1</sup> are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3</a>. In order to activate the electrode material and form a dense SEI film, the battery is precharged at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup> for five cycles. Obviously, the initial discharge capacity and CE of Ni–Se@G@C are 936.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and 71.8%. When the current density is tuned to 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity suddenly reduces to 498.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Then, the discharge capacities of Ni–Se@G@C batteries at the 150th, 300th, 450th, 600th, 750th, 900th, and 1000th cycles are 323.1, 394.3, 518.2, 597.0, 653.7, 650.3, and 626.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Notably, the Ni–Se@G@C battery maintains high CE (&gt;97%) for the remaining cycles, confirming its excellent cycle stability. This is consistent with the high repeatability of the GCD curve. Even after running 1000 cycles, a high discharge capacity of 626.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is delivered. In sharp contrast, the cyclic performance of the NiSe<sub>2</sub> battery is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S3c,d</a>. The initial specific discharge capacity of the NiSe<sub>2</sub> battery is 676.6 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and CE is as low as 70.2%. When the current density is 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity is significantly reduced to 318.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and then it is reduced to 120.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after operating 100 cycles. NiSe<sub>2</sub> shows poor cyclic performance. These results favorably confirm the superiority of the Ni–Se@G@C, stemming from its rational design. The change in the microstructure of Ni–Se@G@C anode after running 1000 cycles is studied. As shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S4a,b</a>, most of the spherical particles still maintain the complete structure without obvious rupture, indicating its good structural integrity. The corresponding elemental mapping spectrum presents uniform distributions of C, N, O, Ni, Se, and P elements. The emergence of the P signal demonstrates the formation of an SEI film containing phosphorus species. TEM and high-resolution TEM (HRTEM) images are shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S5</a>. A rough and clustered structure is observed, caused by the prolonged Li<sup>+</sup> insertion and extraction process. In HRTEM images, the distance of 0.238 nm corresponds to the (211) plane of NiSe<sub>2</sub>. These results indicate that the crystal structure is well preserved.</div><div class="NLM_p">The long-cycle performance of the Ni–Se@G@C battery at 0.1–2 A g<sup>–1</sup> is further evaluated (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>g,h). The initial charge and discharge capacities of the battery are 1218.3 and 843.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, along with a high CE of 89.1%. When the current density is turned to 2 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the specific discharge capacity is 548.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. At the 200th, 400th, 600th, 800th, 1000th, 1200th and 1500th cycles, the charge/discharge capacities are 333.3/332.1, 332.1/328.2, 315.5/314.5, 367.1/362.9, 435.5/429.5, 481.2/476.2 and 458.3/457.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. Of note, CE values are all higher than 98.0%, showing the relatively stable long-cycle performance. The rate capability of the Ni–Se@G@C electrode is evaluated and is plotted in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>e,f. The discharge/charge capacities at 0.1, 0.5, 1, and 2 A g<sup>–1</sup> are 853.3/594.2, 496.9/452.3, 431.8/403.9 and 377.3/348.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. When the current density is increased to 4 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge/charge capacities of Ni–Se@G@C are 314.3/284.3 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> and the CE is 90.4%. When the current density returns to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, a capacity of 434.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is restored, which gradually increases to 582.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. The corresponding capacity retention ratio is 68.3%. In addition, a well-overlapping discharge platform is observed for Ni–Se@G@C at different current densities, confirming its excellent rate performance. We have made the cycling performance comparison <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68 ref69 ref70 ref71 ref72 ref73 ref74'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref58 ref59 ref60 ref61 ref62 ref63 ref64 ref65 ref66 ref67 ref68 ref69 ref70 ref71 ref72 ref73 ref74" aria-label="reference 58 to 74">(58−74)</a> with previous works, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig8" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 8</a>. The result indicates a significantly improved cycling property.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Subsequently, EIS plots of the batteries are presented. In the Nyquist curve, there are two overlapping semicircles in the high- and midfrequency regions, with a linear region in the low-frequency zone. The Randles equivalent circuit model used is shown in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>, where (a) is the equivalent circuit model of the fresh battery and (b) is the equivalent circuit model of the recycled battery. Among them, CPE<sub>f</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> correspond to the capacitance and resistance of the SEI film, and CPE<sub>ct</sub> and <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> correspond to double-layer capacitance and charge transfer resistance, respectively. <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> denotes Warburg impedance, which is closely associated with ion diffusion. <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> is the ohmic internal resistance of the battery material. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref75'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref75" aria-label="reference 75">(75)</a> As illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig9" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 9</a>, the fresh NiSe<sub>2</sub> electrode has an <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 198.9 Ω, while a much lower <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 47.41 Ω is found for Ni–Se@G@C. The highly conductive carbon structure in the Ni–Se@G@C electrode contributes to the promotionsin electrical conductivity and mechanical stability. In addition, the volume expansion effect of the anode during the charge/discharge process is inhibited. We also estimate the resistance variation of Ni–Se@G@C and NiSe<sub>2</sub> cells when operating at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref76 ref77'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref76 ref77" aria-label="reference 76 and 77">(76,77)</a> <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of NiSe<sub>2</sub> at the 30th, 80th, 130th, 180th, 230th, and 280th cycles are 44.7, 35.0, 60.9, 30.7, 37.5, and 29.2 Ω, respectively. In contrast, <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of Ni–Se@G@C at the 30th, 80th, 130th, 180th, 230th, and 280th cycles are 41.7, 22.2, 24.3, 23.2, 18.6, and 14.8 Ω, respectively. This reflects that Ni–Se@G@C has better reaction kinetics than NiSe<sub>2</sub>.</div></div><div id="sec3_4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i13" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i13">3.4.  Li<sup>+</sup> Reaction Kinetics and Storage Mechanism</h3><div class="NLM_p">Based on CV curves at different sweep speeds (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>a), the Li<sup>+</sup> storage behavior and the corresponding reaction kinetics of the Ni–Se@G@C cell are studied. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref78'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref78" aria-label="reference 78">(78)</a> The colored areas in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>a represent the total energy storage for Faraday and non-Faraday actions. The relationship between peak current (<i>i</i>) and sweep speed (<i>v</i>) is shown as follows:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq3"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-103"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-104"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-105"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-106"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-107">i</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-108"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-109" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-110"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-111">a</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-112"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-113" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-114">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-115" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-116">b</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-4"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>a</mi><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mi>b</mi></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(3)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq4"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-117"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-118"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-119"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-120"><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-121">log</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-122"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-123" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-124">i</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-125" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-126"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-127" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-128"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-129">b</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-130" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0.25em;"></span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-131">log</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-132"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-133" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-134">v</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-135" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-136" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-mi" id="MJXp-Span-137">log</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-138"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-139" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-140">a</span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-141" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">)</span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-5"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>log</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>i</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>b</mi><mo linebreak="nobreak" lspace="0em" rspace="0.25em">⁡</mo><mi>log</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>v</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>+</mo><mi>log</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>a</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(4)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p">When the <i>b</i> value is close to 1, the electrochemical process is dominated by the pseudocapacitance reaction. When the <i>b</i> value is close to 0.5, the electrochemical process is dominated by diffusion. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref79'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref79" aria-label="reference 79">(79)</a> <i>b</i> values of 0.652, 0.858, and 1.013 corresponding to peaks 1–3 are obtained, as illustrated in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig7" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 7</a>b, indicating the mixed lithium storage mechanism. The above results further indicate that the Ni–Se@G@C electrode has partial pseudocapacitance behavior during the redox process, which contributes to the rapid insertion/removal of Li<sup>+</sup>. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref80'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref80" aria-label="reference 80">(80)</a> As is well-known, the lithium storage performance of batteries is influenced by the pseudocapacitance behavior and diffusion effect. The specific contribution values can be calculated using the following formulas:<div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq5"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math MJXp-display" id="MJXp-Span-142"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-143"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-144"><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-145"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-146">i</span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-147"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-148" style="margin-left: 0em; margin-right: 0em;">(</span><span id="MJXp-Span-162">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-163" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-164">2</span></span></span><span id="MJXp-Span-174">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-6"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>V</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(5)</span></div></div><div class="MathJaxEquation" id="eq6"><div class="equation"><span class="MathJax_Preview" style="color: inherit;"><span class="MJXp-math margin-right: 0.111em;">/</span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-185"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-186" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-187">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-188" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-189"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-190">1</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-191"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-192">/</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-193"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-194">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-195"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-196" style="margin-left: 0.333em; margin-right: 0.333em;">=</span><span class="MJXp-mstyle" id="MJXp-Span-197"><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-198"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-199" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-200">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-201" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-202">1</span></span></span><span class="MJXp-msup" id="MJXp-Span-203"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-204" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-205">v</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-206" style="vertical-align: 0.5em;"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-207"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-208">1</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-209"><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-210">/</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-211"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-212">2</span></span></span></span><span class="MJXp-mo" id="MJXp-Span-213" style="margin-left: 0.267em; margin-right: 0.267em;">+</span><span class="MJXp-msub" id="MJXp-Span-214"><span class="MJXp-mrow" id="MJXp-Span-215" style="margin-right: 0.05em;"><span class="MJXp-mi MJXp-italic" id="MJXp-Span-216">k</span></span><span class="MJXp-mrow MJXp-script" id="MJXp-Span-217" style="vertical-align: -0.4em;"><span class="MJXp-mn" id="MJXp-Span-218">2</span></span></span></span></span></span></span><script type="math/mml" id="MathJax-Element-7"><math display="block" indentalign="left"><mrow><mrow><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><mi>i</mi><mrow><mo stretchy="false">(</mo><mi>V</mi><mo stretchy="false">)</mo></mrow><mo>/</mo><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mo>/</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></mrow></msup></mstyle></mrow></mrow><mrow><mo linebreak="nobreak">=</mo><mstyle indentshift="3em" indentshiftlast="3em"><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow></msub><msup><mrow><mi>v</mi></mrow><mrow><mrow><mn>1</mn></mrow><mrow><mo>/</mo></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></mrow></msup><mo>+</mo><msub><mrow><mi>k</mi></mrow><mrow><mn>2</mn></mrow></msub></mstyle></mrow></math></script></div><div class="label"><span class="labelSpan">(6)</span></div></div></div><div class="NLM_p last">Specifically, <i>i</i>(<i>V</i>) represents the total current, <i>k</i><sub>1</sub><i>v</i> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub><i>v</i><sup>1/2</sup> represent the current stemming from pseudocapacitance and diffusion actions at a fixed potential, respectively. <i>k</i><sub>1</sub> and <i>k</i><sub>2</sub> represent the slopes and intercepts at different points. <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>c shows the fitted CV curve at 0.6 mV s<sup>–1</sup>, and the pseudocapacitance (red area) is reckoned to be 83.99%. As the scan rate increases, the contribution value of the pseudocapacitance gradually increases, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig10" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 10</a>d.</div></div><div id="sec3_5" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i18" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i18">3.5.  Thermal Hazard of SIBs</h3><div class="NLM_p last">We further investigated the thermal safety of SIBs. The corresponding parameters are shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig11" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 11</a> and <a class="ref showTableEvent internalNav" data-id="tbl2" href="#tbl2">Table 2</a>. The Ni–Se@G@C cell shows <i>T</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> of 264.4 and 305.2 °C at 870.6 and 1114.1 min, respectively. In contrast, the graphite cell gives <i>T</i><sub>0</sub> and <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> values of 175.2 and 284.1 °C at 320.1 and 588.6 min, respectively. Clearly, <i>T</i><sub>0</sub>, <i>T</i><sub>1</sub>, <i>t</i><sub>0</sub>, and <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> are improved significantly. It is noteworthy that <i>T</i><sub>2</sub> of the Ni–Se@G@C cell is 2.7 °C higher than that of the graphite cell. In addition, the Ni–Se@G@C battery presents an HRR<sub>max</sub> of 2.4 °C/min, while the graphite cell portrays an HRR<sub>max</sub> of 8.3 °C/min. The value of <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> always represents the difficulty of TR. The higher the <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> is, the higher the thermal safety of the cell is. The Ni–Se@G@C cell shows an <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> of 1.06 eV, which is much higher than that of the graphite cell (0.16 eV). These results all strongly confirm the efficacy of the Ni–Se@G@C anode in improving battery safety. The radar map of TR parameters (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig12" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 12</a>) of half cells strongly corroborates the superior safety of SIBs with a Ni–Se@G@C anode.</div></div><div id="sec3_6" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i19" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i19">3.6.  Electrochemical Evaluation of SIBs</h3><div class="NLM_p">Sodium storage electrochemical properties of Ni–Se@G@C are also investigated. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig13" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 13</a>a,b, when the current density is 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the initial discharge capacity of the Ni–Se@G@C cell can reach 651.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, giving a CE of 85.8%. A steady cycling behavior is observed for the Ni–Se@G@C cell. Concretely, this cell delivers capacities of 415.2, 420.9, 463.8, 500.4, and 525.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 20th, 40th, 60th, 80th, and 100th cycles, respectively. At the same time, the CE remains at approximately 99.0%. The increase in capacity can be attributed to the gradual activation of active substances and the formation of a polymer gel-like SEI film. In addition, the cyclic performance of the Ni–Se@G@C electrode under 0.1–1 A g<sup>–1</sup> is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig13" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 13</a>e,f. The discharge/charge capacities at the first cycle are 624.9/529.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, giving a CE of 84.0%. When the current density is adjusted to 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the discharge capacity suddenly decreases to 442.2 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Moreover, the cell gives capacities of 308.3, 276.6, 280.1, 276.9, and 269.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 40th, 80th, 120th, 160th, and 200th cycles.</div><div class="NLM_p"><a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig13" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 13</a>c,d shows the electrochemical performance of the cell at 0.1–0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup>. The discharge/charge capacities of the Ni–Se@G@C cell in first cycle are 617.9/529.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, and the CE is maintained at approximately 100% in subsequent cycles. Clearly, capacity declines are observed in the first 100 cycles. Specifically, the cell shows capacities of 330.1 and 318.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at the 30th and 50th cycles, respectively. Subsequently, a capacity increase is observed. After running 100, 150, and 200 cycles, the capacity reaches 320.1, 349.1, and 362.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Even after 250 cycles, a high capacity of 342.8 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> is still maintained. In contrast, the NiSe<sub>2</sub> cell gives an original discharge capacity of 331.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which reduces to 99.7 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> after running 200 cycles (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S7</a>). As for the Ni–Se@G@C cell, the capacity at the 100th cycle is reduced by 29.0% compared with the capacity at the sixth cycle. With regard to the NiSe<sub>2</sub> cell, a reduction of 62.0% is observed. Such results indicate the superior capacity retention ability of Ni–Se@G@C. Comparison with reported works (<a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig14" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 14</a>) further demonstrates the advantage of Ni–Se@G@C in promoting the cycling performance of SIBs. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref81 ref82 ref83 ref84 ref85 ref86 ref87 ref88 ref89'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref81 ref82 ref83 ref84 ref85 ref86 ref87 ref88 ref89" aria-label="reference 81 to 89">(81−89)</a> The rate performance of the Ni–Se@G@C cell is shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig13" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 13</a>g,h. The discharge capacities of 601.7, 406.6, 359.3, 332.1, and 306.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> are discerned at 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2, and 4 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In sharp contrast, the NiSe<sub>2</sub> cell shows poor performance. Specifically, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig13" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 13</a>i, the discharge capacities of the NiSe<sub>2</sub> cell are 238.9, 218.2, 204.2, 189.8, and 13.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1, 0.5, 1, 2, and 4 A g<sup>–1</sup>, respectively. In addition, the NiSe<sub>2</sub> cell shows more significant polarization at high rates, resulting in serious capacity attenuation. When the current returns to 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the Ni–Se@G@C battery still delivers a capacity of 400.0 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. However, the capacity of the NiSe<sub>2</sub> battery is close to 0, which is attributed to severe damage to the NiSe<sub>2</sub> structure during high-rate tests.</div><div class="NLM_p last">Electrochemical impedance spectra (EIS) tests for Ni–Se@G@C and NiSe<sub>2</sub> batteries after the rate test are studied. The related profiles and data are listed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig15" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 15</a>. In Nyquist plots, there are two partially overlapping semicircles in high- and medium-frequency regions with a linear plot in the low-frequency zone. Randles equivalent circuit models are drawn in <a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">Figure S6</a>. Concretely, CPE<sub>f</sub> and CPE<sub>ct</sub> represent the capacitance of SEI film and double-layer capacitance, while <i>R</i><sub>f</sub> relates to the resistance of the SEI film and charge-transfer resistance. Also, <i>R</i><sub>e</sub> signifies the ohmic internal resistance of the cells. <i>Z</i><sub>w</sub> is the Warburg impedance, intimately associated with ion diffusion. The Ni–Se@G@C battery has an <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 17.23 Ω, while the NiSe<sub>2</sub> cell gives an <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of 32.7 Ω. In addition, the Ni–Se@G@C battery has a higher slope than the NiSe<sub>2</sub> battery, indicating its low ion diffusion impedance. EIS results of cells at the 20th, 40th, and 60th cycles at 0.1–0.3 A g<sup>–1</sup> are further tested, as shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig15" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 15</a>c–e. The semicircle at high frequency indicates the difficulty of the charge transfer. That is, the larger the semicircle shape is, the more difficult the charge transfer is, leading to a more difficult electrochemical process. <a onclick="showRef(event, 'ref90'); return false;" href="javascript:void(0);" class="ref ref90" aria-label="reference 90">(90)</a> It can be seen that the semicircle of the Ni–Se@G@C battery at low frequency is much smaller than that of NiSe<sub>2</sub>. By fitting the EIS curve, the <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> of the NiSe<sub>2</sub> battery under different cycles can be obtained. The values of the NiSe<sub>2</sub> battery at the 20th, 40th, and 60th cycles are 131.8, 15.79, and 68.06 Ω, respectively. <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values of the Ni–Se@G@C battery at the 20th, 40th, and 60th cycles are 3.859, 8.059, and 15.28 Ω, respectively. Such markedly decreased <i>R</i><sub>ct</sub> values contribute to the high performance of the Ni–Se@G@C battery.</div></div><div id="sec3_7" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_2"><div id="ac_i20" class="anchor-spacer"></div><h3 class="article-section__title" id="_i20">3.7.  Na<sup>+</sup> Reaction Kinetics and Storage Mechanism</h3><div class="NLM_p last">According to CV curves at different sweep rates, Na<sup>+</sup> reaction kinetics and storage mechanism of the Ni–Se@G@C cell are studied. The related results are listed in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig16" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 16</a>. With the increase of sweep rate, the area of the CV curve gradually increases, but its shape remains similar. The minor position changes in redox peaks indicate the excellent electrochemical stability and low voltage polarization of the Ni–Se@G@C cell. According to <a class="ref internalNav" href="#eq4" aria-label="eq 4">eq 4</a>, <i>b</i> values at peaks 1–5 are 0.528, 0.425, 1.424, 1.390, and 0.748, respectively, which confirms the existence of pseudocapacitance behavior. In <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig16" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 16</a>c, the contribution value of the pseudocapacitance (red area) is 66.89% at 0.4 mV s<sup>–1</sup>. As shown in <a data-tab="pane-pcw-Figures" href="#fig16" id="" class=" internalNav">Figure 16</a>d, the contribution value of the pseudocapacitance increases gradually with the elevation of the scan rate.</div></div></div><div id="sec4" class="NLM_sec NLM_sec_level_1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i21">4.  Conclusion</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><div class="NLM_p last">In this research, a heterogeneous engineering strategy is utilized to acquire in situ carbon-encased metal selenides (Ni–Se@G@C) as anodes for LIBs and SIBs. When used in LIBs, the cell shows a high initial discharge capacity of 936.5 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>, which remains at 626.1 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> even after 1000 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. We have investigated the TR actions of both half and full LIB cells. Compared with the half graphite cell, <i>T</i><sub>1</sub> of the Ni–Se@G@C cell is increased by 87.0 °C. In addition, <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> of the Ni–Se@G@C cell is delayed by 39.6 min. In comparison to the full graphite cell, <i>t</i><sub>0</sub>, <i>t</i><sub>1</sub>, and <i>t</i><sub>2</sub> of the Ni–Se@G@C cell are delayed by 11.5, 28.1, and 28.1 min, respectively. When used in SIBs, the cell shows an initial discharge capacity of 624.9 mAh g<sup>–1</sup> at 0.1 A g<sup>–1</sup>. Even after running 200 cycles at 1 A g<sup>–1</sup>, the cell still delivers a high capacity of 269.4 mAh g<sup>–1</sup>. Notably, the <i>t</i><sub>1</sub> reaction is significantly delayed by 525.5 min. The use of Ni–Se@G@C induces a high TR <i>E</i><sub>a</sub> of 1.06 eV, which is 5.6 times higher than that of the graphite cell. These results jointly confirm the high efficacy of Ni–Se@G@C in promoting electrochemical properties and safety. In short, this work offers inspiration for the superior and safe metal selenides, highlighting their prospects in energy storage.</div></div><div class="NLM_back"><div class="article_supporting-info" id="notes1"><div class="article_content-header"><div class="article_content-header-row"><div class="article_content-title" tabindex="0"><h2 id="_i22">Supporting Information</h2><div class="article__copy"><button class="article__copy__btn"><i class="article__copy__icon icon-copy-solid" aria-hidden="true"></i></button><span class="article__copy__text">Click to copy section link</span><span class="article__copy__success">Section link copied!</span></div></div></div></div><hr><p class="last">The Supporting Information is available free of charge at <a href="/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c09246?goto=supporting-info" class="ext-link">https://pubs.acs.org/doi/10.1021/acsami.4c09246</a>.</p><ul class="NLM_list-list_type-label" id="silist1"><li><p class="inline">EDS mapping of Ni–Se@G@C, XRD pattern of Ni–Se@G@C, cycling performances and GCD curves, SEM and mapping of Ni–Se@G@C after running 1000 cycles, TEM and HRTEM of Ni–Se@G@C after running 1000 cycles, Randles equivalent circuit models, cycling performance and GCD curves of NiSe<sub>2</sub> cell (<a href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf" class="ext-link">PDF</a>)</p></li></ul><p></p><div class="supInfoBoxOnFTP "><div class="sup-info-attachments"><ul><li><a class="suppl-anchor" href="/doi/suppl/10.1021/acsami.4c09246/suppl_file/am4c09246_si_001.pdf">am4c09246_si_001.pdf (952.18 kb)</a></li></ul></div></div></div><div class="testing" data-doi="10.1021/acsami.4c09246" data-url="https://widgets.figshare.com/static/figshare.js" id="figshare-widget" style="display: block;"></div><div class="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3" data-pb-dropzone="TermsAndConditionsDropzone3"><div data-widget-def="UX3HTMLWidget" data-widget-id="85b54935-30e9-4058-a6d2-0b40fb754a85" class="mb-4">

                        cas_cit1R"><div class="casContent"><span class="casTitleNuber">1</span><div class="casTitle"><span class="NLM_cas:atitle">From Metal-Organic Framework to Li2S@C-Co-N Nanoporous Architecture: A High-Capacity Cathode for Lithium-Sulfur Batteries</span></div><div class="casAuthors">He, Jiarui; Chen, Yuanfu; Lv, Weiqiang; Wen, Kechun; Xu, Chen; Zhang, Wanli; Li, Yanrong; Qin, Wu; He, Weidong</div><div class="citationInfo"><span class="NLM_cas:title">ACS Nano</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:date">2016</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:volume">10</span>
        (<span class="NLM_cas:issue">12</span>),
    <span class="NLM_cas:pages">10981-10987</span>CODEN:
                <span class="NLM_cas:coden">ANCAC3</span>;
        ISSN:<span class="NLM_cas:issn">1936-0851</span>.
    
            (<span class="NLM_cas:orgname">American Chemical Society</span>)
        </div><div class="casAbstract">Owing to the high theor. specific capacity (1166 mAh/g), lithium sulfide (Li2S) has been considered as a promising cathode material for Li-S batteries.  However, the polysulfide dissoln. and low electronic cond. of Li2S limit its further application in next-generation Li-S batteries.  In this report, a nanoporous Li2S@C-Co-N cathode is synthesized by liq. infiltration-evapn. of ultrafine Li2S nanoparticles into graphitic carbon co-doped with cobalt and nitrogen (C-Co-N) derived from metal-org. frameworks.  The obtained Li2S@C-Co-N architecture remarkably immobilizes Li2S within the cathode structure through phys. and chem. mol. interactions.  Owing to the synergistic interactions between C-Co-N and Li2S nanoparticles, the Li2S@C-Co-N composite delivers a reversible capacity of 1155.3 (99.1% of theor. value) at the initial cycle and 929.6 mAh/g after 300 cycles, with nearly 100% Coulombic efficiency and a capacity fading of 0.06% per cycle.  It exhibits excellent rate capacities of 950.6, 898.8, and 604.1 mAh/g at 1C, 2C, and 4C, resp.  Such a cathode structure is promising for practical applications in high-performance Li-S batteries.</div></div><div class="moreSciFi"><a title="Retrieve detailed record, substances, reactions and more for this article from SciFinder ®" href="https://scifinder.cas.org/scifinder/view/link_v1/reference.jsf?l=BmxxGlm8wGr5vZ3CT78qUrVg90H21EOLACvtfcHk0lhwDqGM03HAcA"> &gt;&gt; More from SciFinder <sup>®</sup></a></div><span class="casRefUrl">https://chemport.cas.org/services/resolver?origin=ACS&amp;resolution=options&amp;coi=1%3ACAS%3A528%3ADC%252BC28XitVSrsbvM&amp;md5=3b85ecbab0c20c5cc3f3f2fbcf1b7e08</span></div></div></div></div></li><li id="ref2"><div class="reference"><strong class="refLabel"><a href="javascript:void(0);" onclick="showRef(event, 'ref2'); return false;" data-citation="" class="refNumLink">2</a></strong><div class="NLM_citation references__item" id="cit2" data-doi="10.1021/nl401952h"><span><span class="NLM_contrib-group"><span class="NLM_string-name">Lee, S.
